```
I am always interested in how people learn stuff and problems they 
encounter.

I also found OO concepts "hard" when I first tried to grapple with them 
(after 30 years of procedural programming using mainly COBOL).

 Using OO COBOL did NOT make it easier.

Fortunately, I decided it might be good to learn Java about that time so I 
decided to do that. As I so often do (did the same with C# and VS 2005) I 
pretended I knew nothing and approached it as an Absolute Beginner.

I don't measure what I'm trying to learn against what I already know 
(ITSA... it's a...) or have any EXPECTATIONS as to how it OUGHT to work... I 
write onto a clean slate.

Also fortunately, I picked what was (for me) the perfect book to start Java 
with. Written by an insightful, capable, and very witty man, with a wicked 
sense of humour, my Java learning progressed in leaps and bounds.

As Java is totally OO, so did my understanding of OO concepts.

I went back to OO COBOL and couldn't understand why I had previously found 
it difficult... :-)

That was about 12 years ago.

I've moved on since then, and I would NEVER go back to procedural coding.

For a number of years I wrote components in OO COBOL.

It was good.

Now I write them in C#.

It's not better... it is LIGHT YEARS better! (Hence, I won't really join the 
"debate" here about OO. For me, it is not debatable...  The proof of the 
pudding is in the eating, and my pudding has become delicious, thank you 
very much :-))

I'm not interested in Batch Processing and I'll be glad when the need for it 
is gone (about 20 years from now...)

I watch the debate here without emotion because I'm beyond feeling anything 
about it.

I see people writing passionately about how unnecessary OO is, as if the 
majority of the programmers in the world are idiots who have accepted a 
gigantic con... Having spent time with quite a few of them, I know they are 
not.:-)

But, at the end of the day, it isn't the OO languages which are in 
decline...

It's a landslide...all the bleating about "Tremor?! What tremor? I felt no 
tremor.... did you feel a tremor???" won't stop the avalanche. Whether you 
felt what caused it or not, if you want to be part of computer programming 
in the 21st century, you better get with the program, literally.

Learn OO. (Or take up blacksmithing, swan upping, fletching, or lace 
making...all respectable and skilled trades, but totally irrelevant to the 
modern world.)

Pete.
```

```
"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
> Judson McClendon wrote:
>>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> actually seem to run faster than their "all in one script" counterparts, and the CPU usage looks the same.

Scripting is inherently inefficient too, possibly accounting for most of
the overhead in that situation.

>> As
>> has been pointed out by others in these threads, there just is not good
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> I don't know - it's really helped me.  Maybe it's just something I "get" (or that OO helps me "get").

Perhaps so; you're not the only one to express that. We're all different.

>> In the 1970s I spearheaded implementing the relatively new structured
>> methodologies at my shop. Within a year I had solid hard data to prove,
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> smart-alecky...  I tried 5 different ways of wording it, and that was the best I could come up with.)

No, I'm pretty satisifed with the actual code I'm producing right now,
as to clarity and effectiveness. I've become pretty comfortable in OO,
as far as getting it done.

My biggest concern is that the overall people cost of learning and using
OO is far too great; the ROI is insufficient. OO effectively eliminates
the word "simple" from the process.

Believe me, I don't have a problem with complexity, when it's warranted.
I once wrote a series of reports that had a section so complex that, even
heavily commented, I put a heading over the code something to the effect
"Abandon all hope, ye who enter here." Several years later, a programmer
under a different IS manager decided to modify the code. Naturally, she
paid no attention to my warnings, broke the code and screamed "Help!"
The IS manager called to chew me out. But after I explained the system
requirements that his predecessor had given me, why I had done it that
way, and that I had provided and documented an easy and simple way to
accomplish such changes, he backed off and chewed out his programmer for
ignoring my warnings. I once wrote a compiler and P-code interpreter in
COBOL for a utility program I marketed, because COBOL was the only
compiler available to me across the target platforms. I also wrote a
graphics library in COBOL, from dot plot to line and character draw, and
graphics data compression algorithms, including an assembly language
printer driver on the receiving end so I could send 8 bit binary graphics
data from an EBCDIC mainframe across a 7 bit ASCII connection to an 8 bit
graphics laser printer to print ultrasound obstetrics charts. When I
tried to explain how that all worked to the client, his eyes glazed over.
My point is that I've often dealt with complexity, enjoy solving complex
problems, when the complexity is for very good reason. IMO, OO is not a
sufficient reason for this level of complexity.

Perhaps a more balanced approach than the "anything not an object is Bad"
approach would be better. Just because recursive logic can be wonderfully
simple, elegant and even beautiful for certain things, wo don't chuck
iterative out the window; because it's better for even more things. IOW,
don't throw the baby out with the bathwater.

Again and again, as I encounter new things in OO, once I research how to
approach it, I can't help but think how I would have done the same thing
using structured procedural techniques, and am stunned by how much more
complex the "everything OO or die" approach is. And this impression is
born out by the lack of good data to show that OO makes the process any
easier, quicker, or less expensive. Show me the definitive data, and I'll
admit my impressions are wrong. Since the data backs up my impressions,
I'll continue to believe I'm right. :-)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Java is becoming the new Cobol

- **From:** tim Josling <tejgcc_nospam@westnet.com.au>
- **Date:** 2008-01-10T20:58:06
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13od1muitt3hofd@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<28b05ca0-c7a7-4ad5-b307-644da0ffc5fa@h11g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <NoednW8wwYa-heLaRVn_vwA@giganews.com> <lAKfj.62062$K27.47894@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <FdidnWXlad8m3x3anZ2dnUVZ_oCvnZ2d@comcast.com> <0t5gj.59398$rc2.24476@bignews1.bellsouth.net> <2cWdnUoouf_CPRzanZ2dnUVZ_ternZ2d@comcast.com> <FVpgj.66851$L%6.5193@bignews3.bellsouth.net> <6IqdnXzvIcNDrBnanZ2dnUVZ_sOrnZ2d@comcast.com> <fV4hj.44124$Mu4.7226@bignews7.bellsouth.net> <5umh13F1iqnuaU1@mid.individual.net> <N3qhj.30175$L27.28676@bignews9.bellsouth.net>`

```
On Thu, 10 Jan 2008 08:36:29 -0600, Judson McClendon wrote:

> You have yet to supply any *hard data* to show your productivity and
> accuracy is better with OO, you have only supplied subjective
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> white, on paper, clear for anyone to see.

Past paradigm shifts were accompanied by hard data eg machine code to
assembler, assembler to high level languages, structured programming.

The switch to OO has not been accompanied by good data to show benefits.

Consider one of the few studies that has been done:

http://page.mi.fu-berlin.de/prechelt/Biblio/jccpprt2_advances2003.pdf

Note that the C++ and Java programs were slower than the C programs *and*
took longer to develop. This is consistent with my experience that OO code
often does struggle with performance. OO projects, especially Java-based
projects, are often horrendously slow and expensive.

Richard Gabriel reports in Patterns of Software that even a year after
conversion from Lisp to C++ ie functional to OO programming, the
productivity was down 30% across the whole project life cycle. The initial
hit was far higher, and this was with an elite group of programmers.

I see three problems at least

1. The fact is that much of reality is not well-modelled using the
paradigm "nouns with verbs attached" which is the OO paradigm. Part of the
problem is trying to shoe-horn problem spaces that don't fit OO into the
OO paradigm.

2. OO languages tend to become very complex due to inheritance,
overloading of operators, virtual methods, attempts to reduce the impact
of static typing (via function overloading), and attempts to reintroduce
macros while pretending not to do so (templates etc). These issues
introduce a blizzard of hard to understand corner cases and obscure bugs.

Java in a Nutshell is now 1,000 pages long. The cognitive demands and the
number of things one needs to memorise create a learning curve and a
barrier to entry, as others have reported here.

3. The tendency to reify everything as objects means that things that
should be in databases end up as classes and objects. The result is that
lots of things are hard-coded and the resulting system is inflexible and
hard to maintain.

If there was any evidence that OO improved productivity they would be
shouting it from the rooftops.

With approaches that actually do offer productivity benefits, the evidence
is out there:

http://norvig.com/java-lisp.html

And see also the original study above for productivity benefits of
languages with dynamic typing. It is interesting that these benefits seem
independent of whether the languages supports OO.

Tim Josling
```

```
On Thu, 10 Jan 2008 20:14:21 -0500, Charles Hottel wrote:

> Interesting but aslo dated by 4-5 years during which time java performance 
> has steadily improved. There is also a Java optimiser and native code 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Undoubtedly scripting languages have their place.

Even with the claimed 4X speedup from java -client that's barely on a par
with native C. And with a tiny toy benchmark (checking the sign of an
integer) that is not a realistic test.

I would be more convinced to see say specint or specfp in Java
running anywhere near C speeds. As a rule Java is 3X slower than a HLL
like C and C or COBOL is 3X slower than assembler. With C/COBOL versus
assembler there is a benefit.

The main issue is not runtime speed, but development productivity. As you
pointed out, that includes maintenance. I haven't seen any signs that Java
maintenance fares any better than start from scratch development though.

To the (surprisingly significant) extent that lines of code is an
indication, we can expect Java will fare poorly. Because of the large
amounts of boilerplate code required to do even simple things.

Tim Josling
```

```
If you look at most of the texts on Java / OO they are full of examples
like this. 

Class regular-geometric-shape
	field length-of-side.

Class square subclasses regular-geometric-shape...

Class triangle subclasses regular-geometric-shape...

Class pentagon subclasses regular-geometric-shape...

Class hexagon subclasses regular-geometric-shape...

etc.

IMHO these subclasses would be better modelled as a single class with some
data saying how many sides. This makes it a lot easier to add say
octagons.
```

```

I see lots of hard coding from people who find re-use of programmer
supplied objects to be less safe than they want.   They don't want to
modify someone else's code and then test all of the applications that
access that someone else's code.  (Maybe modifying the test
procedures).   Accepting that the automated tests still test all of
the functionality (when that functionality is in another part of the
company's business from yours) can get people into trouble.

Solution?   Ignore the benefits of reusability - keep isolated.
```

```
top-post p.s. - Wow, this got long.  Sorry...

tim Josling wrote:
> On Fri, 11 Jan 2008 07:23:08 -0700, LX-i wrote:
> 
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> octagons.

I agree with your analysis of this.  In fact, what I'd do is make a 
second class..

public class GeometricShapeType {
     public static final Long TRIANGLE = 3L;
     public static final Long SQUARE = 4L;
     public static final Long PENTAGON = 5L;
     public static final Long HEXAGON = 6L;
     public static final Long OCTAGON = 8L;
}

public class RegularGeometricShape {

     protected Long mlLengthOfSide;
     protected Long mlNumberOfSides;

     // By default, we have a 4-length square.
     public RegularGeometricShape() {
         this.setNumberOfSides(GeometricShapeType.SQUARE);
         this.setLengthOfSide(4L);
     }

     // Create a shape specified by the caller.
     public RegularGeometricShape(
             final Long plShapeType, final Long plSideLength) {
         this.setNumberOfSides(plShapeType);
         this.setLengthOfSide(plSideLength);
     }

     public boolean isShape(final Long plShapeType) {
         return this.getNumberOfSides().equals(plShapeType);
     }

     // get and set methods redacted for space...
}

Then, in the classes that actually use this...

public class UseShapes {

     public void doSomething() {
         RegularGeometricShape oStopSign = new RegularGeometricShape(
                 GeometricShapeType.OCTAGON, 18L);

         if (oStopSign.isShape(GeometricShapeType.SQUARE)) {
             System.out.println("It is a square");
         }
         else {
             System.out.println("It is not a square");
         }
     }
}

Granted, a lot of that *is* hard-coded, but then again, shapes are 
hard-coded by the rules of geometry.  :)  I think that this example 
(from the textbook) is more oriented at people who don't even know 
computers at all.  None of these analogies worked for me - shapes, types 
of cars, etc.  It was actually seeing it in action in a real system that 
helped me "get it".

(Here's where the quotes are messed up...)

=-=-= quote =-=-=

Class bank-account

Class checking-account subclasses bank-account
Class savings-account subclasses bank-account.

=-=-= end quote =-=-=

I could see this.  Where the real choice needs to be made is in what 
common data these accounts share, and if anything (other than type) is 
different.  In analyzing this example (and, you banking folks, don't 
laugh at me - I've only done aircraft maintenance and safety 
reporting!), I would break it down as follows...

"BankAccount"
  - Account holder (either a key or an Customer object)
  - Current balance (number to two decimals)
  - Interest rate (number to five(?) decimals)
  - Transaction history (possibly not part of the object)

Do checking and/or savings accounts need more information that that?  I 
can't think of anything.  I alluded to breaking up objects earlier this 
week.  Let me go into how I did that, and maybe it'll make more sense. 
It also serve as the basis for addressing issues in the next part of 
your post.  (I'm not revealing anything that anyone inside a safety 
career field wouldn't know.)

We have a "Data Extraction Tool" in our system, which allows users to 
select data from mishap reports given several different criteria.  Some 
of these include mishap date, safety discipline (aviation, ground, 
etc.), fiscal year, base name, organization, severity, objects, etc. 
They can also do a word search on the various narratives that are 
entered by the investigators.  The user can name the query, schedule it 
to run on a recurring basis, and e-mail the results to them.  The output 
can be in HTML, Word, or Excel.  For Excel, the user can select the 
columns that they want in the output.

This entire thing was contained in one object - let's call it 
MishapQuery.  (I wrote it myself - just the properties, constructors, 
getters, and setters were bigger than some COBOL programs I've worked on 
in the past!)  It was big, but it did what it needed to do.

Now, we're developing occupational illness investigation capabilities, 
and they want the same data extraction capabilities as the mishap 
investigations want.  Additionally, they also want to be able to pull 
this information in the format required by OSHA.  Of course, the input 
criteria is different, and the information for the HTML, Excel, and Word 
output is different.

To do this, I looked at the existing MishapQuery object, and determined 
that there were three "groups" of data being stored there...

1) Identification information (name, auto-execute, e-mail)
2) Input options
3) Output options (HTML, Word, Excel)

 From looking at the requirements, though, the OSHA report is a monthly 
report, with a fixed output format.  What this led me to is a three 
tiers of inheritance...

BaseQuery - This holds the identification portion of the query 
(including query "type" - mishap, illness, OSHA, etc.).
BaseReportQuery extends BaseQuery - This adds word-search capability and 
HTML, Word, and Excel output specifics.

Which leads to...
MishapQuery extends BaseReportQuery - A query on mishap investigations.
IllnessQuery extends BaseReportQuery - A query on illness investigations.
OshaQuery extends BaseQuery - A query on illness investigations, 
returned in the format that OSHA requires (and it has no word-search 
capability or output options).

I don't think this is "hard-coding", just a natural progression.  As an 
aside, the routine that handles the automatic execution now only has to 
address the BaseQuery, as that's all the information it needs to know 
whether to kick it off or not.

=-=-= quote =-=-=

If you think about where data should live, there is a hierarchy:

 From most performance critical and least likely to change, to least
performance critical and most likely to change.

1. Put in hardware
2. Hard code
3. Put in fast in-memory look-up table
4. Put in a database.

In the days of procedural coding, there was a lot of use of fast
in-memory tables. When done right, this made for very flexible systems.

=-=-= end quote =-=-=

How would this be used to define functionality for a system?  For 
example, we have a "matrix" in our system, which is cached (so it's "in 
memory" most of the time), that validates the information in a report. 
It is smart enough to know when each field is required, based on defined 
rules and the other sorts of data entered.  Would this be something like 
what you're describing?

And, as far as database goes, each object has an empty constructor, and 
one that takes a DataObject (an abstraction object that represents a row 
of data from the database).  So, the constructors look something like...

public BaseQuery(DataObject oData) {
     this.setProperty(oData.getLong("property"));
     // etc.
}

public BaseReportQuery(DataObject oData) {
     super(oData);
     this.setWordSearch(oData.getString("word_search"));
     // etc.
}

public MishapQuery(DataObject oData) {
     super(oData);
     this.setDiscipline(oData.getString("discipline"));
     // etc.
}

Additionally, we have "services" (some folks would call them "helpers") 
that are used to instantiate the object and save changes to the objects. 
  In this case, it creates the object, and populates the options.  These 
are ArrayList objects, and you can't really get them with a single 
query.  For example, the MishapQueryService has a method like the 
following...

public MishapQuery getMishapQuery(final Long plQueryId) {

     MishapQuery oQuery;

     if (NumberUtils.nullOrZero(plQueryId)) {
         oQuery = new MishapQuery();
         oQuery.setExcelOptions(getDefaultExcelOptions);
     }
     else {
         // get data in a try/catch block
         oQuery = new MishapQuery(oData[0]);
         oQuery.setExcelOptions(getExcelOptions(plQueryId));
     }

     return oQuery;
}

So, at least in our case, we're utilizing the database heavily (at least 
the way I see it).  :)

[snip]

=-=-= quote =-=-=

Possibly one reason that this happens is that the text books are all full
of bad examples such as those above, where things are hard-coded that
should not be hard-coded.

=-=-= end quote =-=-=

Like I said above, those "textbook" examples were actually a hindrance 
to me.  Even now that I know OO, I can see the limitations of such 
analogies.

=-=-= quote =-=-=

Another explanation I have heard is that the OO world, being full of
young programmers who are not yet sick of churning out thousands of lines
of code, and who have not yet dealt with the legacy of huge amounts of
inflexible code, has just not reached a point of maturity where the
use of in-memory tables is very appealing.

Maybe the problem is that putting this data in tables does not feel right
because tables are not objects.

=-=-= end quote =-=-=

Sure - blame the kids!  ;)  But, I do have a question - would you put 
the geometric shapes in tables?

I haven't even gotten into some of the other objects we have.  The ones 
I like are "TableMatic", "ListMatic", and "TreeMatic".  They are generic 
classes that take a set of data and some XML configuration, and render 
tables, lists, and trees with standard attributes.  (For example, unless 
explicitly forbidden in the configuration, all columns in the table are 
sortable.)

I'm not saying that hard-coding doesn't exist - I'm just trying to 
determine whether I lucked into a shop that "does it right," or if I'm 
not seeing things the way you do.
```

```
IMHO this is still too much hard coding. I would probably buy "number of
sides" as a field, not hard coding the different X-agons.

> =-=-= quote =-=-=
> 
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>   - Transaction history (possibly not part of the object)

Last time I did this, I identified seventeen different types of balances
alone. A typical account would have a hundred or so fields that are unique
to that instance, plus fields (usually tables) that are common to accounts
of that type (eg interest rate tiers).

Here are a few
- date opened
- stopped to debits flag
- days overdrawn
- accrued interest positive / negative
- statement frequency, next due date
- Basel II compliance data
- IRS related data

> We have a "Data Extraction Tool" in our system, which allows users to
> select data from mishap reports given several different criteria.  

Wouldn't it be easier to dump the data into a data mart and give them a
query tool?

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> the geometric shapes in tables?

I really have noticed that there are a lot of people out there who just
love writing code and whether it is the sensible thing to do does not seem
to be an issue for them.

As for would I put shapes in a DBMS, it would depend on the problem space.
But let's say the shapes are ones you would call for from a palette, I
thing they should as far as possible be in data not code. The code should
be used to provide capabilities which are then invoked by the data. Maybe
the users should be allowed to create their own shapes, within the limits
of the capabilities available.

> I'm not saying that hard-coding doesn't exist - I'm just trying to
> determine whether I lucked into a shop that "does it right," or if I'm
> not seeing things the way you do.
>

It would seem that you did luck out.

The test is how costly development, testing and maintenance are, and how
easy it is to change things. Are customers frustrated because of the
cost and time involved in providing "trivial" new features.


There is nothing fundamental about in-memory tables. They are just a
performance tweak on top of a database. But if done right, they can make
it feasible to move things out of code and into data, thus providing a lot
of flexibility.

Tim Josling
```

```
tim Josling wrote:
> Sorry about the lines that messed up your reader.

It's all good...  :)  It reminded me of how I used to have to put quotes 
together on FidoNet...  heh...

>>> IMHO these subclasses would be better modelled as a single class with some
>>> data saying how many sides. This makes it a lot easier to add say
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> sides" as a field, not hard coding the different X-agons.

This class is simply a convenience class, with constants used to allow 
more "readable" (I know, that's subjective) code.  The "number of sides" 
was a field, just in the GeometricShape class, not the 
GeometricShapeType class.  IOW, this allows you to say

new GeometricShape(GeometricShapeType.SQUARE);

instead of

new GeometricShape(4L);

Of course, it doesn't *prohibit* the latter.  I suppose you could have a 
table instead, where you would say something like

new GeometricShape(Shape.getDimensions("square"));

, where you had something like

class Shape {

     protected ArrayList<String, Long> maDimensions;

     public static Long getDimensions(final String psShape) {
         if (ArrayUtils.nullOrEmpty(maDimensions)) {
             // populate array list from database or an
             // in-memory cache.
         }
         return maDimensions.getValue(psShape);
     }
}

>> =-=-= quote =-=-=
>>
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> - IRS related data

So are you saying that you *would* subclass an account based on type, or 
you wouldn't?  :)

>> We have a "Data Extraction Tool" in our system, which allows users to
>> select data from mishap reports given several different criteria.  
> 
> Wouldn't it be easier to dump the data into a data mart and give them a
> query tool? 

One would think.  Ridiculous amounts of money lately, we have nothing. 
A couple of members of our team are getting smart on that sort of thing, 
as the public data warehouse folks seem to have trouble with our data.

>> =-=-= quote =-=-=
>> Another explanation I have heard is that the OO world, being full of
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> of the capabilities available.

I could see that.  :)

> The test is how costly development, testing and maintenance are, and how
> easy it is to change things. Are customers frustrated because of the
> cost and time involved in providing "trivial" new features.

No, not really.  The occupational illness folks just sat through a demo 
of what we've done for them, and were really impressed with the way the 
application works.  Data was validated, and there was even a process to 
check for missing data.

We did it so quickly because we could re-use lots of building blocks 
from the mishap reporting module.  :)  Things like a person's name and 
contact information and demographic info use the exact same rules.  And, 
where they had new Lists of Values (LOVs), we already had the AJAX base 
laid for populating tier 2 from tier 1, tier 3 from tier 2, etc. - it 
was just a matter of writing the new query and sending it to the class.
```

```
Ahhhhh, the Oldene Dayse, Mr Josling... leaving aside the quibble about 
Thou Shalt Not Hardcode PROCEDURE DIVISION values' I recall having to 
include the question of 'how much data'?  After all, an in-core table is 
limited to 32K... unless you line them up one after the other and use an 
'overflowing' subscript.

(I can almost smell the greybeards out there smiling as they read this... 
as for those who never needed the technique, Life is Good.  For those who 
heard '*No* programmer worth his salary needs more than 16K of core!'... 
Life is Even Better.)

When asked 'how would you design...' my immediate response is 'that 
depends on anticipated data volume'... and if some kind - *any* kind! - of 
answer was to be found it was doubled and used as a base-line.
```

```
On Sat, 12 Jan 2008 01:42:39 +0000, docdwarf wrote:

> Ahhhhh, the Oldene Dayse, Mr Josling... leaving aside the quibble about 
> Thou Shalt Not Hardcode PROCEDURE DIVISION values' I recall having to 
> include the question of 'how much data'?  After all, an in-core table is

> 'overflowing' subscript.

We had (have!) in core tables of many megabytes. They had to be moved over
the 16mb line because they wouldn't find below it. These are
tables with descriptions written in a COBOL-like language and the data
stored in an RDBMS. For a new table, no code needed to be written, other
than the calls to use it.

Tim Josling
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Java is becoming the new Cobol

- **From:** tim Josling <tejgcc_nospam@westnet.com.au>
- **Date:** 2008-01-12T19:38:47
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13oi5q7dt8qcve8@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<28b05ca0-c7a7-4ad5-b307-644da0ffc5fa@h11g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13offrpnknc146@corp.supernews.com> <fm95uf$k30$1@reader2.panix.com> <13ogspbk4kbmn9c@corp.supernews.com> <fmakfv$48i$1@reader2.panix.com>`

```
On Sat, 12 Jan 2008 14:57:03 +0000, docdwarf wrote:


>>We had (have!) in core tables of many megabytes.
> 
> How interesting... and how were those defined in ANSI '74 COBOL?
> 
> DD

This was COBOL 85. Actually the tables were used by COBOL programs but
were implemented in assembler.

There was a program which took descriptions of the tables, in a COBOL-like
language, and generated assembler programs to access the tables. The
tables themselves were generated as rmode31 modules out of the RDBMSm
using other programs. These programs generated assembler modules from the
tabular data in the RDBMS.

Tim Josling
```

- **References:** `<28b05ca0-c7a7-4ad5-b307-644da0ffc5fa@h11g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <NoednW8wwYa-heLaRVn_vwA@giganews.com> <lAKfj.62062$K27.47894@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <FdidnWXlad8m3x3anZ2dnUVZ_oCvnZ2d@comcast.com> <0t5gj.59398$rc2.24476@bignews1.bellsouth.net> <2cWdnUoouf_CPRzanZ2dnUVZ_ternZ2d@comcast.com> <FVpgj.66851$L%6.5193@bignews3.bellsouth.net> <6IqdnXzvIcNDrBnanZ2dnUVZ_sOrnZ2d@comcast.com> <fV4hj.44124$Mu4.7226@bignews7.bellsouth.net> <5umh13F1iqnuaU1@mid.individual.net> <N3qhj.30175$L27.28676@bignews9.bellsouth.net> <5unla9F1i4131U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
>> Two years after going structured, I could have buried you in data as
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> So why didn't the World stay with Structured Programming?

I believe it was a combination of things. But primarily I believe it was
that, as the Internet became vastly more important (also GUI), and new
paradigms were needed to address them, OO was also becoming popular among
the academics. People needing solutions were convinced by the academics
that OO was a good general solution, not only to the new requirements, but
everything under the sun. Unfortunately, going "whole hog OO" is, IMHO,
the most expensive mistake ever made in computer science.

It comes from making such decisions based on judgement from those who
learned everything they know from books and classes taught by people who
also learned everything they know from books and classes, and so on.
Consider, Microsoft was one of the Big Pushers for OO, and one of the
biggest "winners" in terms of profiting from the rush to OO. But only a
few years ago the average age for Microsoft employees was under 20! I do
not think the same decisions would have been made by people who also had
strong practical experience. Education increases knowledge. But experience
improves judgment, which can't be leaned from books and classes.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Java is becoming the new Cobol

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2008-01-09T08:14:42-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<j0o9o3hfk5ptrcvoejgl0aolelf1lgrq2t@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<28b05ca0-c7a7-4ad5-b307-644da0ffc5fa@h11g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <NoednW8wwYa-heLaRVn_vwA@giganews.com> <lAKfj.62062$K27.47894@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <FdidnWXlad8m3x3anZ2dnUVZ_oCvnZ2d@comcast.com> <0t5gj.59398$rc2.24476@bignews1.bellsouth.net> <2cWdnUoouf_CPRzanZ2dnUVZ_ternZ2d@comcast.com> <FVpgj.66851$L%6.5193@bignews3.bellsouth.net> <6IqdnXzvIcNDrBnanZ2dnUVZ_sOrnZ2d@comcast.com>`

```
On Tue, 08 Jan 2008 19:31:03 -0700, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:

>Well, yeah.  Any VM is going to be more inefficient, especially if 
>things aren't run repetitively.  Most VMs cache the "compiled" version 
>of the interpreted intermediate language, so the first execution is 
>horrid, but future ones are much better.

Looking at different types of VMs, I see old mainframes using one
type, and have this interesting comparisons on using Windows on a Mac:
http://www.mactech.com/articles/mactech/Vol.24/24.02/VirtualizationBenchmark/

I would have figured that Boot Camp (booting straight into Windows)
would be considerably faster than the two VM products running Windows
within OS-X, but that's not what they found.

Of course, both products were designed to run on the same CPU -
whereas Java was designed independently of the actual hardware (I
remember reading about someone creating a Java chip).    But this
still illustrates that efficiencies can be made that can change our
evaluations.

Heck, even with CoBOL, costs have changed so that it's more cost
effective to make code easier to maintain relative to quicker to run.
And some of our coding efficiencies can be irrelevant with some system
changes (the caches you mentioned above).
```

- **References:** `<28b05ca0-c7a7-4ad5-b307-644da0ffc5fa@h11g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <NoednW8wwYa-heLaRVn_vwA@giganews.com> <ietun3hsk4uiug3ilj27mcr0ll7t3k8p0t@4ax.com> <zIKfj.62066$K27.55895@bignews6.bellsouth.net>`

```


"Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote in message 
news:zIKfj.62066$K27.55895@bignews6.bellsouth.net...
> "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
>> Scott <NoSpam@Spamblocker.org> wrote:
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> hadn't even been born.

ARPANET and scholastic networks like JANET were running from the late 
60s.(First live ARPANET node, september, 1969 at UCLA; JANET evolved from a 
number of earlier nets dating back to mid 70s, but first went live as JANET 
in 1983, with 50 nodes connected.)While the Internet may have been 
technically born in 1991, objects were around long before that. XEROX SPARC 
and Smalltalk are the accepted inception of what we now recognise as 
"Objects",  and the most widely used implementation of Smalltalk was 
released in 1980, but similar functionality was available in the mid 70s. 
Xerox Palo Alto had been experimenting with these concepts in the mid 70s 
and they were not the only ones.

Creationism cannot be applied to the Internet, although there may be a case 
for "intelligent design"...:-)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Java is becoming the new Cobol

```
Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote in 
news:ietun3hsk4uiug3ilj27mcr0ll7t3k8p0t@4ax.com:

> It's about allowing a student to connect his laptop computer to the
> Web, log on, and sign up for a class.   It's about allowing a salesman
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> The functionality is more than just the pretty face.


It is about a pretty face WITH functionality and you cannot have 
a pretty face on the mainframe. Companies always want the pretty screens
and the mainframe loses because of it, in spite of its clear advantage in
almost every other area. 

I'm sorry to say but as a Systems Analyst, over and over again, users
and management are always more interested in how an application LOOKS
than in how it WORKS. Of course if it doesn't work right there is a big
problem. But the point is, in every phase of an application life cycle,
to the people outside of I.T. who hold the purse strings, LOOKING good
is always their first priority. And to THEM, looking good MEANS 
functionality. That's what screws the mainframe. 

It may have cost IBM a pretty penny years ago to make graphic screens 
but what is it costing them now? Short-sighted corporate mentality 
loses the business again.
```

```

> It is about a pretty face WITH functionality and you cannot have
> a pretty face on the mainframe.

We are a Unisys and an IBM shop, and on the Unisys side we are
starting to use ePortal, which is a app that acts as an web interface
replacing BOLTS (or at least duplicating it, hopefully replacing it at
some point!). It's essentially a screen scraper.

I haven't worked with ePortal or even seen it personally, but I've
heard it's 'easy' to make a screen and presents a very pretty face,
apparently, if you have some basic web/html skills.

I think the only real advantage (other than security), other than
allowing access to administrative screens from anywhere on the Web, is
that it allows a greater use of the mouse and looks nicer to the folks
who are used to web interfaces.

Having worked quite a bit with BOLTS the last year, (almost) ANYTHING
that replaces that is a good thing, as long as it just doesn't
duplicate the effort needed and actually replaces it.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Java is becoming the new Cobol

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2008-01-05T12:20:50-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<pUPfj.5192$OC1.1484@newsfe20.lga>`
- **References:** `<28b05ca0-c7a7-4ad5-b307-644da0ffc5fa@h11g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <NoednW8wwYa-heLaRVn_vwA@giganews.com>`

```

Scott <NoSpam@Spamblocker.org> wrote in message
news:NoednW8wwYa-heLaRVn_vwA@giganews.com...
> THE biggest problem with the mainframe is that IBM never embraced a GUI
> screen. That's what it's all about. Why is everyone migrating from
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It's easy, of course, to be wise after the fact.  At the time GUI's didn't
exist and nobody except a few wizards at Palo Alto knew anything about them.
In fact, when PC's first became generally available - IBM, remember - there
was a good deal of skepticism about their general usefulness; "real"
computers those days had a big data entry department, and nobody really
thought that users would be willing to take on that burden.  They were, of
course, in the long run.  And it wasn't the GUI that made PC's take off; it
was the introduction of the first popular spreadsheet that made it happen -
Visicalc, I think? - but not so much because of the application; I myself
wrote a crude batch-oriented spreadsheet-like program about 1979; it was the
realization that the screen could be made infinitely extensible in any
direction that was the breakthough.  Until then nearly everybody had written
programs that fit strictly on the screen!  It's impossible to predict with
any certainty what will be a breakthrough.

There was a time when IBM's continued existence was a topic for considerable
debate.  There was also no lack of helpful advice.  One innocent soul
recommended that IBM scrap all its operating systems and implement Windows
on them.  Like that was going to happen!

Although I haven't worked with IBM's mainframes for a long time I'm certain
that there's a way of wrapping GUIs around the green-screen applications
thereby giving the users the rich experience they want.

I question just how rich an experience users actually want.  I know I get
very tired of plowing through all the fancy stuff when I have a specific
goal in mind: and I get extremely mad and take my business elsewhere when a
vendor tells me my browser isn't supported.  My most recent such experience
was with an airline; in my innocence I though the only requirement to fly
with them was to have enough money; but no, all its customers had to have
the very latest browser as well.  So far as I'm concerned, the appearance is
a very poor second to the functionality, and if that means a duller site and
the lowest-common-denominator coding then so be it.

PL
```

```
"tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in news:pUPfj.5192$OC1.1484@newsfe20.lga:

I gave the same argument for years. It just isn't what management and 
user's want I have finally realized. To the people who hold the purse
strings and make the decisions, graphic screens are "new", 3270 screens
are "old" and they want "new technology" not "old technology" even if it
ISN'T better. 

As a programmer and analyst I agree with you that functionality and 
performance should come first. But appearance is what they want and
they associate that with functionality. To the decision makers,
functionality MEANS drop down boxes, scroll bars, pictures and graphics,
point and click. That's what they want, they have the money and the power,
so they put everything on Windows or UNIX and get rid of the mainframe.
Real functionality, maintainability, performance, security, reliability
and so on is not something they understand anyway.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Java is becoming the new Cobol

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-01-05T20:02:59-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bp80o3dvb67erlrnlgl0iltmag8ut0kjvd@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<28b05ca0-c7a7-4ad5-b307-644da0ffc5fa@h11g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <NoednW8wwYa-heLaRVn_vwA@giganews.com> <pUPfj.5192$OC1.1484@newsfe20.lga>`

```
On Sat, 5 Jan 2008 12:20:50 -0600, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>
>Scott <NoSpam@Spamblocker.org> wrote in message
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>thought that users would be willing to take on that burden. 

When the IBM PC appeared in 1982, I KNEW it was a revolution. I bought one with $2,000 of
my own money. A few months later I bought several with the company's money and moved half
its programmrs to developing PC software. 

One of the first things I did, as proof of concept, was a drag race between a small
mainframe, 4361 with 3310 disks running VSE, versus a 4.77 MHz PC with a slow 10 M disk.
The application was my idea of typical batch processing against an indexed/VSAM file. The
PC won!!

The first application I wrote, in compiled Basic and a little assembly, had an audio user
interface over the telephone. It said push 1 for this, 2 for that. Its basic function was
receiving supermarket orders from handheld Telxon computers that talked through 1200 baud
acoustic couplers. When the user pushed 1 to send an order, the PC turned off a
voice/DTMF modem and turned on a 1200 baud data modem .. without dropping the telephone
connection. At the end of the order, it reversed the switch and spoke a hash total that
the user compared to the hash total on his screen. Other functions involved checking the
status of orders. The store manager could call from his home phone to check the various
departments in his store. The response would say 'Grocery order due at 6PM, received at
5:17. Meat order due at 5PM, not received yet. Produce order due at 6AM tomorrow.'

That's easy today, with off the shelf hardware. In 1983, all we had was analog to digital
converters. We had to digitize the speech ourselves, write our own audio editors and feed
the speech sounds to the telephone. 

The application multi-tasked under MSDOS. Each machine could simultaneously handle four
phone lines and eight modems. In production, the company had eight machines connected to
32 phone lines. After I left in 1986, one of my guys rewrite it in Realia Cobol, which
made it run 2-3 times faster. It ran in production for ten years. 

>They were, of
>course, in the long run.  And it wasn't the GUI that made PC's take off; it
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>any certainty what will be a breakthrough.

In 1988 Lucid had the breakthrough idea that spreadsheets could be three dimentional. Our
(I worked there) Lucid 3D won the PC Magazine award for best application, and the idea was
promptly copied by Lotus and Microsoft. 

The feature still exists in Excel, but isn't widely used (I could be wrong). It wasn't
such a breakthrough after all. Importing and exporting data to csv and XML was a better
idea. 

>There was a time when IBM's continued existence was a topic for considerable
>debate.  There was also no lack of helpful advice.  One innocent soul
>recommended that IBM scrap all its operating systems and implement Windows
>on them.  Like that was going to happen!

Unix would have been an excellent suggestion. Users could have kept old iron instead of
replacing it with Sun and HP. Of course, IBM would have been forced to lower the price by
50-90%. As it happened, they lost 100%.

>Although I haven't worked with IBM's mainframes for a long time I'm certain
>that there's a way of wrapping GUIs around the green-screen applications
>thereby giving the users the rich experience they want.

Many have tried. The problem is that the underlying app is state-oriented. It's expecting
the user to enter one field. 

>I question just how rich an experience users actually want.  I know I get
>very tired of plowing through all the fancy stuff when I have a specific
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>the lowest-common-denominator coding then so be it.

Tell them you want backward compatibility to the green screen. Let us know what they say. 

Better yet, tell them you want the text mode SABRE user interface that was once the norm
with travel agents. It was the most arcane, cryptic thing I've ever seen, and I've seen
some pretty bad ones. Ugly interfaces like that are designed by programmer types sitting
in conference rooms, smugly referring to "dumb users." Then they act hurt because users
abandoned them for flash and eye candy. If they'd designed the interface for the
convenience of users, rather than programmers and bureaucrats, they'd still be happily
married to users.
```

```
Fortunately the Idiot could catch on without my making special mods to 
anything... but the Interloper was kind of upset that the Idiot got good

reality, cause him wasted time and annoyance.
```

```
(I remember talking to the CEO of a major UK utility and asking why they 
were sending out letters to customers without signatures and on green 
lineflo... A multi-million dollar system, that couldn't do what a PC or Mac 
that cost less than a thousandth of the price could do all day long standing 
on its head, as a fundamental principle.)

Given that people (Users) actually WANTED a GUI (or at least, something more 
intuitive than a command line...) interface, don't you think it is incumbent 
on us as IT professionals to try and address that need?

Mainframes CAN do GUI, they just need a different approach from the 
imperative COBOL one. And the IT community was not about to accept that. 
(Judging from this forum, they still aren't...:-))

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Mainframe and GUI was Re: Java is becoming the new Cobol

- **From:** Clark Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2008-01-09T22:13:39-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<qsuao3ti15kjo9g1m4gfdtbpcs4v6han4h@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<28b05ca0-c7a7-4ad5-b307-644da0ffc5fa@h11g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <NoednW8wwYa-heLaRVn_vwA@giganews.com> <5uahisF1h18c7U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Sun, 6 Jan 2008 12:18:49 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
>on its head, as a fundamental principle.)

Don't blame the mainframe, blame the IT management.  We had a Xerox
laser printer in my shop in the 1980's.  It had a megabyte of memory
and I think a 100 megabyte disk.  It printed on both sides of the
paper and if we had gotten fancy, I think we had our choice of fonts.
I suspect we could have printed signatures if needed.
>
>Given that people (Users) actually WANTED a GUI (or at least, something more 
>intuitive than a command line...) interface, don't you think it is incumbent 
>on us as IT professionals to try and address that need?

Most users in my shop were going into CICS which was constrained by
being on a text terminal but they certainly were not dealing with a
command line.  The help system provided was non-existent but that was
more because management didn't see the need to take advantage of the
help capabilities provided by CICS.  The programmers were using ISPF
and definitely not the command line interface since management didn't
want them to get outside of ISPF capabilities.  No one was dealing
with a command line interface.
>
>Mainframes CAN do GUI, they just need a different approach from the 
>imperative COBOL one. And the IT community was not about to accept that. 
>(Judging from this forum, they still aren't...:-))

The infrastructure wasn't there until IBM got serious about Java and
Websphere.  If the main interactive application was CICS, OO wasn't
going to help that much until the appropriate infrastructure was
available.  I understand that there are C/C++ classes for dealing with
CICS.  If they are in source to be included at compile that won't help
COBOL but if they are in a form to be either dynamically invoked or
linked in at link time, theoretically they could be made available to
COBOL programs if IBM would get off its COBOL will only talk to JAVA
jag.  
>
>Pete.
```
